<Section>1</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="2">So shaken as we are, so wan with care,</Line>
<Line number="3">Find we a time for frighted peace to pant,</Line>
<Line number="4">And breathe short-winded accents of new broils</Line>
<Line number="5">To be commenced in strands afar remote.</Line>
<Line number="6">No more the thirsty entrance of this soil</Line>
<Line number="7">Shall daub her lips with her own children's blood;</Line>
<Line number="8">Nor more shall trenching war channel her fields,</Line>
<Line number="9">Nor bruise her flowerets with the armed hoofs</Line>
<Line number="10">Of hostile paces: those opposed eyes,</Line>
<Line number="11">Which, like the meteors of a troubled heaven,</Line>
<Line number="12">All of one nature, of one substance bred,</Line>
<Line number="13">Did lately meet in the intestine shock</Line>
<Line number="14">And furious close of civil butchery</Line>
<Line number="15">Shall now, in mutual well-beseeming ranks,</Line>
<Line number="16">March all one way and be no more opposed</Line>
<Line number="17">Against acquaintance, kindred and allies:</Line>
<Line number="18">The edge of war, like an ill-sheathed knife,</Line>
<Line number="19">No more shall cut his master. Therefore, friends,</Line>
<Line number="20">As far as to the sepulchre of Christ,</Line>
<Line number="21">Whose soldier now, under whose blessed cross</Line>
<Line number="22">We are impressed and engaged to fight,</Line>
<Line number="23">Forthwith a power of English shall we levy;</Line>
<Line number="24">Whose arms were moulded in their mothers' womb</Line>
<Line number="25">To chase these pagans in those holy fields</Line>
<Line number="26">Over whose acres walk'd those blessed feet</Line>
<Line number="27">Which fourteen hundred years ago were nail'd</Line>
<Line number="28">For our advantage on the bitter cross.</Line>
<Line number="29">But this our purpose now is twelve month old,</Line>
<Line number="30">And bootless 'tis to tell you we will go:</Line>
<Line number="31">Therefore we meet not now. Then let me hear</Line>
<Line number="32">Of you, my gentle cousin Westmoreland,</Line>
<Line number="33">What yesternight our council did decree</Line>
<Line number="34">In forwarding this dear expedience.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="35">My liege, this haste was hot in question,</Line>
<Line number="36">And many limits of the charge set down</Line>
<Line number="37">But yesternight: when all athwart there came</Line>
<Line number="38">A post from Wales loaden with heavy news;</Line>
<Line number="39">Whose worst was, that the noble Mortimer,</Line>
<Line number="40">Leading the men of Herefordshire to fight</Line>
<Line number="41">Against the irregular and wild Glendower,</Line>
<Line number="42">Was by the rude hands of that Welshman taken,</Line>
<Line number="43">A thousand of his people butchered;</Line>
<Line number="44">Upon whose dead corpse there was such misuse,</Line>
<Line number="45">Such beastly shameless transformation,</Line>
<Line number="46">By those Welshwomen done as may not be</Line>
<Line number="47">Without much shame retold or spoken of.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="50">This match'd with other did, my gracious lord;</Line>
<Line number="51">For more uneven and unwelcome news</Line>
<Line number="52">Came from the north and thus it did import:</Line>
<Line number="53">On Holy-rood day, the gallant Hotspur there,</Line>
<Line number="54">Young Harry Percy and brave Archibald,</Line>
<Line number="55">That ever-valiant and approved Scot,</Line>
<Line number="56">At Holmedon met,</Line>
<Line number="57">Where they did spend a sad and bloody hour,</Line>
<Line number="58">As by discharge of their artillery,</Line>
<Line number="59">And shape of likelihood, the news was told;</Line>
<Line number="60">For he that brought them, in the very heat</Line>
<Line number="61">And pride of their contention did take horse,</Line>
<Line number="62">Uncertain of the issue any way.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="63">Here is a dear, a true industrious friend,</Line>
<Line number="64">Sir Walter Blunt, new lighted from his horse.</Line>
<Line number="65">Stain'd with the variation of each soil</Line>
<Line number="66">Betwixt that Holmedon and this seat of ours;</Line>
<Line number="67">And he hath brought us smooth and welcome news.</Line>
<Line number="68">The Earl of Douglas is discomfited:</Line>
<Line number="69">Ten thousand bold Scots, two and twenty knights,</Line>
<Line number="70">Balk'd in their own blood did Sir Walter see</Line>
<Line number="71">On Holmedon's plains. Of prisoners, Hotspur took</Line>
<Line number="72">Mordake the Earl of Fife, and eldest son</Line>
<Line number="73">To beaten Douglas; and the Earl of Athol,</Line>
<Line number="74">Of Murray, Angus, and Menteith:</Line>
<Line number="75">And is not this an honourable spoil?</Line>
<Line number="76">A gallant prize? ha, cousin, is it not?</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="79">Yea, there thou makest me sad and makest me sin</Line>
<Line number="80">In envy that my Lord Northumberland</Line>
<Line number="81">Should be the father to so blest a son,</Line>
<Line number="82">A son who is the theme of honour's tongue;</Line>
<Line number="83">Amongst a grove, the very straightest plant;</Line>
<Line number="84">Who is sweet Fortune's minion and her pride:</Line>
<Line number="85">Whilst I, by looking on the praise of him,</Line>
<Line number="86">See riot and dishonour stain the brow</Line>
<Line number="87">Of my young Harry. O that it could be proved</Line>
<Line number="88">That some night-tripping fairy had exchanged</Line>
<Line number="89">In cradle-clothes our children where they lay,</Line>
<Line number="90">And call'd mine Percy, his Plantagenet!</Line>
<Line number="91">Then would I have his Harry, and he mine.</Line>
<Line number="92">But let him from my thoughts. What think you, coz,</Line>
<Line number="93">Of this young Percy's pride? the prisoners,</Line>
<Line number="94">Which he in this adventure hath surprised,</Line>
<Line number="95">To his own use he keeps; and sends me word,</Line>
<Line number="96">I shall have none but Mordake Earl of Fife.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

<Line number="113">Thou art so fat-witted, with drinking of old sack</Line>
<Line number="114">
and unbuttoning thee after supper and sleeping upon
</Line>
<Line number="115">benches after noon, that thou hast forgotten to</Line>
<Line number="116">demand that truly which thou wouldst truly know.</Line>

<Line number="118">day? Unless hours were cups of sack and minutes</Line>
<Line number="119">
capons and clocks the tongues of bawds and dials the

<Line number="120">

</Line>

</Line>
<Line number="122">

</Line>

<PlainText>
<Line number="124">
Indeed, you come near me now, Hal; for we that take
</Line>
<Line number="125">purses go by the moon and the seven stars, and not</Line>
<Line number="126">
by Phoebus, he,'that wandering knight so fair.' And,
</Line>
<Line number="127">I prithee, sweet wag, when thou art king, as, God</Line>

<PlainText>
<Line number="284">Well, for two of them, I know them to be as</Line>
<Line number="285">true-bred cowards as ever turned back; and for the</Line>
<Line number="286">
third, if he fight longer than he sees reason, I'll
</Line>
<Line number="287">
forswear arms. The virtue of this jest will be, the
</Line>
<Line number="288">
incomprehensible lies that this same fat rogue will
</Line>
<Line number="289">tell us when we meet at supper: how thirty, at</Line>
<Line number="290">
least, he fought with; what wards, what blows, what
</Line>
<Line number="291">extremities he endured; and in the reproof of this</Line>
<Line number="292">lies the jest.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Section>1</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="298">I know you all, and will awhile uphold</Line>
<Line number="299">The unyoked humour of your idleness:</Line>
<Line number="300">Yet herein will I imitate the sun,</Line>
<Line number="301">Who doth permit the base contagious clouds</Line>
<Line number="302">To smother up his beauty from the world,</Line>
<Line number="303">That, when he please again to be himself,</Line>
<Line number="304">Being wanted, he may be more wonder'd at,</Line>
<Line number="305">By breaking through the foul and ugly mists</Line>
<Line number="306">Of vapours that did seem to strangle him.</Line>
<Line number="307">If all the year were playing holidays,</Line>
<Line number="308">To sport would be as tedious as to work;</Line>
<Line number="309">But when they seldom come, they wish'd for come,</Line>
<Line number="310">And nothing pleaseth but rare accidents.</Line>
<Line number="311">So, when this loose behavior I throw off</Line>
<Line number="312">And pay the debt I never promised,</Line>
<Line number="313">By how much better than my word I am,</Line>
<Line number="314">By so much shall I falsify men's hopes;</Line>
<Line number="315">And like bright metal on a sullen ground,</Line>
<Line number="316">My reformation, glittering o'er my fault,</Line>
<Line number="317">Shall show more goodly and attract more eyes</Line>
<Line number="318">Than that which hath no foil to set it off.</Line>
<Line number="319">I'll so offend, to make offence a skill;</Line>
<Line number="320">Redeeming time when men think least I will.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="323">My blood hath been too cold and temperate,</Line>
<Line number="324">Unapt to stir at these indignities,</Line>
<Line number="325">And you have found me; for accordingly</Line>
<Line number="326">You tread upon my patience: but be sure</Line>
<Line number="327">I will from henceforth rather be myself,</Line>
<Line number="328">Mighty and to be fear'd, than my condition;</Line>
<Line number="329">Which hath been smooth as oil, soft as young down,</Line>
<Line number="330">And therefore lost that title of respect</Line>
<Line number="331">Which the proud soul ne'er pays but to the proud.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Section>1</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="354">My liege, I did deny no prisoners.</Line>
<Line number="355">But I remember, when the fight was done,</Line>
<Line number="356">When I was dry with rage and extreme toil,</Line>
<Line number="357">Breathless and faint, leaning upon my sword,</Line>
<Line number="358">
Came there a certain lord, neat, and trimly dress'd,
</Line>
<Line number="359">Fresh as a bridegroom; and his chin new reap'd</Line>
<Line number="360">Show'd like a stubble-land at harvest-home;</Line>
<Line number="361">He was perfumed like a milliner;</Line>
<Line number="362">And 'twixt his finger and his thumb he held</Line>
<Line number="363">A pouncet-box, which ever and anon</Line>
<Line number="364">He gave his nose and took't away again;</Line>
<Line number="365">Who therewith angry, when it next came there,</Line>
<Line number="366">Took it in snuff; and still he smiled and talk'd,</Line>
<Line number="367">And as the soldiers bore dead bodies by,</Line>
<Line number="368">He call'd them untaught knaves, unmannerly,</Line>
<Line number="369">To bring a slovenly unhandsome corse</Line>
<Line number="370">Betwixt the wind and his nobility.</Line>
<Line number="371">With many holiday and lady terms</Line>
<Line number="372">He question'd me; amongst the rest, demanded</Line>
<Line number="373">My prisoners in your majesty's behalf.</Line>
<Line number="374">I then, all smarting with my wounds being cold,</Line>
<Line number="375">To be so pester'd with a popinjay,</Line>
<Line number="376">Out of my grief and my impatience,</Line>
<Line number="377">Answer'd neglectingly I know not what,</Line>
<Line number="378">He should or he should not; for he made me mad</Line>
<Line number="379">To see him shine so brisk and smell so sweet</Line>
<Line number="380">And talk so like a waiting-gentlewoman</Line>
<Line number="381">
Of guns and drums and wounds,--God save the mark!--
</Line>
<Line number="382">And telling me the sovereign'st thing on earth</Line>
<Line number="383">Was parmaceti for an inward bruise;</Line>
<Line number="384">And that it was great pity, so it was,</Line>
<Line number="385">This villanous salt-petre should be digg'd</Line>
<Line number="386">Out of the bowels of the harmless earth,</Line>
<Line number="387">Which many a good tall fellow had destroy'd</Line>
<Line number="388">So cowardly; and but for these vile guns,</Line>
<Line number="389">He would himself have been a soldier.</Line>
<Line number="390">This bald unjointed chat of his, my lord,</Line>
<Line number="391">I answer'd indirectly, as I said;</Line>
<Line number="392">And I beseech you, let not his report</Line>
<Line number="393">Come current for an accusation</Line>
<Line number="394">Betwixt my love and your high majesty.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="395">The circumstance consider'd, good my lord,</Line>
<Line number="396">Whate'er Lord Harry Percy then had said</Line>
<Line number="397">To such a person and in such a place,</Line>
<Line number="398">At such a time, with all the rest retold,</Line>
<Line number="399">May reasonably die and never rise</Line>
<Line number="400">To do him wrong or any way impeach</Line>
<Line number="401">What then he said, so he unsay it now.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Section>1</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="402">Why, yet he doth deny his prisoners,</Line>
<Line number="403">But with proviso and exception,</Line>
<Line number="404">That we at our own charge shall ransom straight</Line>
<Line number="405">His brother-in-law, the foolish Mortimer;</Line>
<Line number="406">Who, on my soul, hath wilfully betray'd</Line>
<Line number="407">The lives of those that he did lead to fight</Line>
<Line number="408">Against that great magician, damn'd Glendower,</Line>
<Line number="409">Whose daughter, as we hear, the Earl of March</Line>
<Line number="410">Hath lately married. Shall our coffers, then,</Line>
<Line number="411">Be emptied to redeem a traitor home?</Line>
<Line number="412">Shall we but treason? and indent with fears,</Line>
<Line number="413">When they have lost and forfeited themselves?</Line>
<Line number="414">No, on the barren mountains let him starve;</Line>
<Line number="415">For I shall never hold that man my friend</Line>
<Line number="416">Whose tongue shall ask me for one penny cost</Line>
<Line number="417">To ransom home revolted Mortimer.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Section>1</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="418">Revolted Mortimer!</Line>
<Line number="419">He never did fall off, my sovereign liege,</Line>
<Line number="420">But by the chance of war; to prove that true</Line>
<Line number="421">Needs no more but one tongue for all those wounds,</Line>
<Line number="422">Those mouthed wounds, which valiantly he took</Line>
<Line number="423">When on the gentle Severn's sedgy bank,</Line>
<Line number="424">In single opposition, hand to hand,</Line>
<Line number="425">He did confound the best part of an hour</Line>
<Line number="426">In changing hardiment with great Glendower:</Line>
<Line number="427">Three times they breathed and three times did</Line>
<Line number="428">they drink,</Line>
<Line number="429">Upon agreement, of swift Severn's flood;</Line>
<Line number="430">Who then, affrighted with their bloody looks,</Line>
<Line number="431">Ran fearfully among the trembling reeds,</Line>
<Line number="432">And hid his crisp head in the hollow bank,</Line>
<Line number="433">Bloodstained with these valiant combatants.</Line>
<Line number="434">Never did base and rotten policy</Line>
<Line number="435">Colour her working with such deadly wounds;</Line>
<Line number="436">Nor could the noble Mortimer</Line>
<Line number="437">Receive so many, and all willingly:</Line>
<Line number="438">Then let not him be slander'd with revolt.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

<Line number="439">Thou dost belie him, Percy, thou dost belie him;</Line>
<Line number="440">He never did encounter with Glendower:</Line>
<Line number="441">I tell thee,</Line>
<Line number="442">He durst as well have met the devil alone</Line>
<Line number="443">As Owen Glendower for an enemy.</Line>
<Line number="444">Art thou not ashamed? But, sirrah, henceforth</Line>
<Line number="445">Let me not hear you speak of Mortimer:</Line>
<Line number="446">Send me your prisoners with the speediest means,</Line>
<Line number="447">Or you shall hear in such a kind from me</Line>
<Line number="448">As will displease you. My Lord Northumberland,</Line>
<Line number="449">We licence your departure with your son.</Line>

<PlainText>
<Line number="488">Nay, then I cannot blame his cousin king,</Line>
<Line number="489">That wished him on the barren mountains starve.</Line>
<Line number="490">But shall it be that you, that set the crown</Line>
<Line number="491">Upon the head of this forgetful man</Line>
<Line number="492">And for his sake wear the detested blot</Line>
<Line number="493">Of murderous subornation, shall it be,</Line>
<Line number="494">That you a world of curses undergo,</Line>
<Line number="495">Being the agents, or base second means,</Line>
<Line number="496">The cords, the ladder, or the hangman rather?</Line>
<Line number="497">O, pardon me that I descend so low,</Line>
<Line number="498">To show the line and the predicament</Line>
<Line number="499">Wherein you range under this subtle king;</Line>
<Line number="500">Shall it for shame be spoken in these days,</Line>
<Line number="501">Or fill up chronicles in time to come,</Line>
<Line number="502">That men of your nobility and power</Line>
<Line number="503">Did gage them both in an unjust behalf,</Line>
<Line number="504">As both of you--God pardon it!--have done,</Line>
<Line number="505">To put down Richard, that sweet lovely rose,</Line>
<Line number="506">An plant this thorn, this canker, Bolingbroke?</Line>
<Line number="507">And shall it in more shame be further spoken,</Line>
<Line number="508">That you are fool'd, discarded and shook off</Line>
<Line number="509">By him for whom these shames ye underwent?</Line>
<Line number="510">No; yet time serves wherein you may redeem</Line>
<Line number="511">Your banish'd honours and restore yourselves</Line>
<Line number="512">Into the good thoughts of the world again,</Line>
<Line number="513">Revenge the jeering and disdain'd contempt</Line>
<Line number="514">Of this proud king, who studies day and night</Line>
<Line number="515">To answer all the debt he owes to you</Line>
<Line number="516">Even with the bloody payment of your deaths:</Line>
<Line number="517">Therefore, I say--</Line>
</PlainText>

<Line number="525">If he fall in, good night! or sink or swim:</Line>

<Line number="626">Till he hath found a time to pay us home:</Line>

<Line number="628">To make us strangers to his looks of love.</Line>
</PlainText>

Good morrow, Master Gadshill. It holds current that
</Line>
<Line number="696">I told you yesternight: there's a franklin in the</Line>
<Line number="697">wild of Kent hath brought three hundred marks with</Line>
<Line number="698">him in gold: I heard him tell it to one of his</Line>
<Line number="699">
company last night at supper; a kind of auditor; one
</Line>
<Line number="700">that hath abundance of charge too, God knows what.</Line>
<Line number="701">They are up already, and call for eggs and butter;</Line>
<Line number="702">they will away presently.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="708">What talkest thou to me of the hangman? if I hang,</Line>
<Line number="709">
I'll make a fat pair of gallows; for if I hang, old
</Line>
<Line number="710">Sir John hangs with me, and thou knowest he is no</Line>
<Line number="711">starveling. Tut! there are other Trojans that thou</Line>
<Line number="712">dreamest not of, the which for sport sake are</Line>
<Line number="713">
content to do the profession some grace; that would,
</Line>
<Line number="714">if matters should be looked into, for their own</Line>
<Line number="715">credit sake, make all whole. I am joined with no</Line>
<Line number="716">foot-land rakers, no long-staff sixpenny strikers,</Line>
<Line number="717">
none of these mad mustachio purple-hued malt-worms;
</Line>
<Line number="718">
but with nobility and tranquillity, burgomasters and
</Line>
<Line number="719">great oneyers, such as can hold in, such as will</Line>
<Line number="720">strike sooner than speak, and speak sooner than</Line>
<Line number="721">
drink, and drink sooner than pray: and yet, zounds,
</Line>
<Line number="722">
I lie; for they pray continually to their saint, the
</Line>
<Line number="723">commonwealth; or rather, not pray to her, but prey</Line>
<Line number="724">on her, for they ride up and down on her and make</Line>
<Line number="725">her their boots.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="750">I am accursed to rob in that thief's company: the</Line>
<Line number="751">rascal hath removed my horse, and tied him I know</Line>
<Line number="752">not where. If I travel but four foot by the squier</Line>
<Line number="753">
further afoot, I shall break my wind. Well, I doubt
</Line>
<Line number="754">not but to die a fair death for all this, if I</Line>
<Line number="755">'scape hanging for killing that rogue. I have</Line>
<Line number="756">forsworn his company hourly any time this two and</Line>
<Line number="757">twenty years, and yet I am bewitched with the</Line>
<Line number="758">rogue's company. If the rascal hath not given me</Line>
<Line number="759">medicines to make me love him, I'll be hanged; it</Line>
<Line number="760">could not be else: I have drunk medicines. Poins!</Line>
<Line number="761">Hal! a plague upon you both! Bardolph! Peto!</Line>
<Line number="762">I'll starve ere I'll rob a foot further. An 'twere</Line>
<Line number="763">
not as good a deed as drink, to turn true man and to
</Line>
<Line number="764">leave these rogues, I am the veriest varlet that</Line>
<Line number="765">ever chewed with a tooth. Eight yards of uneven</Line>
<Line number="766">ground is threescore and ten miles afoot with me;</Line>
<Line number="767">
and the stony-hearted villains know it well enough:
</Line>
<Line number="768">
a plague upon it when thieves cannot be true one to another!
</Line>
<Line number="769">[They whistle]</Line>
<Line number="770">Whew! A plague upon you all! Give me my horse, you</Line>
<Line number="771">rogues; give me my horse, and be hanged!</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="857">'But for mine own part, my lord, I could be well</Line>
<Line number="858">
contented to be there, in respect of the love I bear
</Line>
<Line number="859">your house.' He could be contented: why is he not,</Line>
<Line number="860">then? In respect of the love he bears our house:</Line>
<Line number="861">
he shows in this, he loves his own barn better than
</Line>
<Line number="862">he loves our house. Let me see some more. 'The</Line>
<Line number="863">purpose you undertake is dangerous;'--why, that's</Line>
<Line number="864">
certain: 'tis dangerous to take a cold, to sleep, to
</Line>
<Line number="865">drink; but I tell you, my lord fool, out of this</Line>
<Line number="866">nettle, danger, we pluck this flower, safety. 'The</Line>
<Line number="867">
purpose you undertake is dangerous; the friends you
</Line>
<Line number="868">
have named uncertain; the time itself unsorted; and
</Line>
<Line number="869">
your whole plot too light for the counterpoise of so
</Line>
<Line number="870">
great an opposition.' Say you so, say you so? I say
</Line>
<Line number="871">
unto you again, you are a shallow cowardly hind, and
</Line>
<Line number="872">you lie. What a lack-brain is this! By the Lord,</Line>
<Line number="873">our plot is a good plot as ever was laid; our</Line>
<Line number="874">friends true and constant: a good plot, good</Line>
<Line number="875">
friends, and full of expectation; an excellent plot,
</Line>
<Line number="876">very good friends. What a frosty-spirited rogue is</Line>
<Line number="877">
this! Why, my lord of York commends the plot and the
</Line>
<Line number="878">
general course of action. 'Zounds, an I were now by
</Line>
<Line number="879">
this rascal, I could brain him with his lady's fan.
</Line>
<Line number="880">Is there not my father, my uncle and myself? lord</Line>
<Line number="881">
Edmund Mortimer, My lord of York and Owen Glendower?
</Line>
<Line number="882">is there not besides the Douglas? have I not all</Line>
<Line number="883">
their letters to meet me in arms by the ninth of the
</Line>
<Line number="884">next month? and are they not some of them set</Line>
<Line number="885">forward already? What a pagan rascal is this! an</Line>
<Line number="886">infidel! Ha! you shall see now in very sincerity</Line>
<Line number="887">
of fear and cold heart, will he to the king and lay
</Line>
<Line number="888">open all our proceedings. O, I could divide myself</Line>
<Line number="889">and go to buffets, for moving such a dish of</Line>
<Line number="890">skim milk with so honourable an action! Hang him!</Line>
<Line number="891">let him tell the king: we are prepared. I will set</Line>
<Line number="892">forward to-night.</Line>
<Line number="893">[Enter LADY PERCY]</Line>
<Line number="894">
How now, Kate! I must leave you within these two hours.
</Line>
</PlainText>

<Section>2</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="895">O, my good lord, why are you thus alone?</Line>
<Line number="896">For what offence have I this fortnight been</Line>
<Line number="897">A banish'd woman from my Harry's bed?</Line>
<Line number="898">
Tell me, sweet lord, what is't that takes from thee
</Line>
<Line number="899">Thy stomach, pleasure and thy golden sleep?</Line>
<Line number="900">Why dost thou bend thine eyes upon the earth,</Line>
<Line number="901">And start so often when thou sit'st alone?</Line>
<Line number="902">Why hast thou lost the fresh blood in thy cheeks;</Line>
<Line number="903">And given my treasures and my rights of thee</Line>
<Line number="904">To thick-eyed musing and cursed melancholy?</Line>
<Line number="905">In thy faint slumbers I by thee have watch'd,</Line>
<Line number="906">And heard thee murmur tales of iron wars;</Line>
<Line number="907">Speak terms of manage to thy bounding steed;</Line>
<Line number="908">Cry 'Courage! to the field!' And thou hast talk'd</Line>
<Line number="909">Of sallies and retires, of trenches, tents,</Line>
<Line number="910">Of palisadoes, frontiers, parapets,</Line>
<Line number="911">Of basilisks, of cannon, culverin,</Line>
<Line number="912">Of prisoners' ransom and of soldiers slain,</Line>
<Line number="913">And all the currents of a heady fight.</Line>
<Line number="914">Thy spirit within thee hath been so at war</Line>
<Line number="915">And thus hath so bestirr'd thee in thy sleep,</Line>
<Line number="916">That beads of sweat have stood upon thy brow</Line>
<Line number="917">Like bubbles in a late-disturbed stream;</Line>
<Line number="918">And in thy face strange motions have appear'd,</Line>
<Line number="919">Such as we see when men restrain their breath</Line>
<Line number="920">
On some great sudden hest. O, what portents are these?
</Line>
<Line number="921">Some heavy business hath my lord in hand,</Line>
<Line number="922">And I must know it, else he loves me not.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Line number="951">Away,</Line>
<Line number="952">Away, you trifler! Love! I love thee not,</Line>
<Line number="953">I care not for thee, Kate: this is no world</Line>
<Line number="954">To play with mammets and to tilt with lips:</Line>
<Line number="955">We must have bloody noses and crack'd crowns,</Line>
<Line number="956">And pass them current too. God's me, my horse!</Line>
<Line number="957">What say'st thou, Kate? what would'st thou</Line>
<Line number="958">have with me?</Line>
</PlainText>

<Line number="963">Come, wilt thou see me ride?</Line>
<Line number="964">And when I am on horseback, I will swear</Line>
<Line number="965">I love thee infinitely. But hark you, Kate;</Line>
<Line number="966">I must not have you henceforth question me</Line>
<Line number="967">Whither I go, nor reason whereabout:</Line>
<Line number="968">Whither I must, I must; and, to conclude,</Line>
<Line number="969">This evening must I leave you, gentle Kate.</Line>
<Line number="970">I know you wise, but yet no farther wise</Line>
<Line number="971">Than Harry Percy's wife: constant you are,</Line>
<Line number="972">But yet a woman: and for secrecy,</Line>
<Line number="973">No lady closer; for I well believe</Line>
<Line number="974">Thou wilt not utter what thou dost not know;</Line>
<Line number="975">And so far will I trust thee, gentle Kate.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Section>2</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="987">
With three or four loggerheads amongst three or four
</Line>
<Line number="988">score hogsheads. I have sounded the very</Line>
<Line number="989">
base-string of humility. Sirrah, I am sworn brother
</Line>
<Line number="990">to a leash of drawers; and can call them all by</Line>
<Line number="991">their christen names, as Tom, Dick, and Francis.</Line>
<Line number="992">They take it already upon their salvation, that</Line>
<Line number="993">though I be but the prince of Wales, yet I am king</Line>
<Line number="994">
of courtesy; and tell me flatly I am no proud Jack,
</Line>
<Line number="995">
like Falstaff, but a Corinthian, a lad of mettle, a
</Line>
<Line number="996">good boy, by the Lord, so they call me, and when I</Line>
<Line number="997">am king of England, I shall command all the good</Line>
<Line number="998">lads in Eastcheap. They call drinking deep, dyeing</Line>
<Line number="999">
scarlet; and when you breathe in your watering, they
</Line>
<Line number="1000">
cry 'hem!' and bid you play it off. To conclude, I
</Line>
<Line number="1001">am so good a proficient in one quarter of an hour,</Line>
<Line number="1002">
that I can drink with any tinker in his own language
</Line>
<Line number="1003">during my life. I tell thee, Ned, thou hast lost</Line>
<Line number="1004">
much honour, that thou wert not with me in this sweet
</Line>
<Line number="1005">action. But, sweet Ned,--to sweeten which name of</Line>
<Line number="1006">Ned, I give thee this pennyworth of sugar, clapped</Line>
<Line number="1007">
even now into my hand by an under-skinker, one that
</Line>
<Line number="1008">never spake other English in his life than 'Eight</Line>
<Line number="1009">
shillings and sixpence' and 'You are welcome,' with
</Line>
<Line number="1010">
this shrill addition, 'Anon, anon, sir! Score a pint
</Line>
<Line number="1011">of bastard in the Half-Moon,' or so. But, Ned, to</Line>
<Line number="1012">drive away the time till Falstaff come, I prithee,</Line>
<Line number="1013">do thou stand in some by-room, while I question my</Line>
<Line number="1014">
puny drawer to what end he gave me the sugar; and do
</Line>
<Line number="1015">thou never leave calling 'Francis,' that his tale</Line>
<Line number="1016">to me may be nothing but 'Anon.' Step aside, and</Line>
<Line number="1017">I'll show thee a precedent.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="1088">
That ever this fellow should have fewer words than a
</Line>
<Line number="1089">
parrot, and yet the son of a woman! His industry is
</Line>
<Line number="1090">
upstairs and downstairs; his eloquence the parcel of
</Line>
<Line number="1091">a reckoning. I am not yet of Percy's mind, the</Line>
<Line number="1092">Hotspur of the north; he that kills me some six or</Line>
<Line number="1093">seven dozen of Scots at a breakfast, washes his</Line>
<Line number="1094">hands, and says to his wife 'Fie upon this quiet</Line>
<Line number="1095">life! I want work.' 'O my sweet Harry,' says she,</Line>
<Line number="1096">'how many hast thou killed to-day?' 'Give my roan</Line>
<Line number="1097">horse a drench,' says he; and answers 'Some</Line>
<Line number="1098">fourteen,' an hour after; 'a trifle, a trifle.' I</Line>
<Line number="1099">prithee, call in Falstaff: I'll play Percy, and</Line>
<Line number="1100">that damned brawn shall play Dame Mortimer his</Line>
<Line number="1101">
wife. 'Rivo!' says the drunkard. Call in ribs, call in tallow.
</Line>
</PlainText>

</Line>
<Line number="1238">
were masters of their wealth. Mark now, how a plain
</Line>
<Line number="1239">
tale shall put you down. Then did we two set on you
</Line>
<Line number="1240">four; and, with a word, out-faced you from your</Line>
<Line number="1241">
prize, and have it; yea, and can show it you here in
</Line>
<Line number="1242">the house: and, Falstaff, you carried your guts</Line>
<Line number="1243">
away as nimbly, with as quick dexterity, and roared
</Line>
<Line number="1244">
for mercy and still run and roared, as ever I heard
</Line>
<Line number="1245">
bull-calf. What a slave art thou, to hack thy sword
</Line>
<Line number="1246">as thou hast done, and then say it was in fight!</Line>
<Line number="1247">What trick, what device, what starting-hole, canst</Line>
<Line number="1248">thou now find out to hide thee from this open and</Line>
<Line number="1249">apparent shame?</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="1251">By the Lord, I knew ye as well as he that made ye.</Line>
<Line number="1252">
Why, hear you, my masters: was it for me to kill the
</Line>
<Line number="1253">heir-apparent? should I turn upon the true prince?</Line>
<Line number="1254">why, thou knowest I am as valiant as Hercules: but</Line>
<Line number="1255">beware instinct; the lion will not touch the true</Line>
<Line number="1256">prince. Instinct is a great matter; I was now a</Line>
<Line number="1257">coward on instinct. I shall think the better of</Line>
<Line number="1258">myself and thee during my life; I for a valiant</Line>
<Line number="1259">
lion, and thou for a true prince. But, by the Lord,
</Line>
<Line number="1260">lads, I am glad you have the money. Hostess, clap</Line>
<Line number="1261">to the doors: watch to-night, pray to-morrow.</Line>
<Line number="1262">
Gallants, lads, boys, hearts of gold, all the titles
</Line>
<Line number="1263">of good fellowship come to you! What, shall we be</Line>
<Line number="1264">merry? shall we have a play extempore?</Line>
</PlainText>

</Line>
<Line number="1315">not an eagle's talon in the waist; I could have</Line>
<Line number="1316">crept into any alderman's thumb-ring: a plague of</Line>
<Line number="1317">sighing and grief! it blows a man up like a</Line>
<Line number="1318">bladder. There's villanous news abroad: here was</Line>
<Line number="1319">Sir John Bracy from your father; you must to the</Line>
<Line number="1320">court in the morning. That same mad fellow of the</Line>
<Line number="1321">
north, Percy, and he of Wales, that gave Amamon the
</Line>
<Line number="1322">bastinado and made Lucifer cuckold and swore the</Line>
<Line number="1323">devil his true liegeman upon the cross of a Welsh</Line>
<Line number="1324">hook--what a plague call you him?</Line>

<PlainText>
<Line number="1379">Peace, good pint-pot; peace, good tickle-brain.</Line>
<Line number="1380">
Harry, I do not only marvel where thou spendest thy
</Line>
<Line number="1381">
time, but also how thou art accompanied: for though
</Line>
<Line number="1382">the camomile, the more it is trodden on the faster</Line>
<Line number="1383">it grows, yet youth, the more it is wasted the</Line>
<Line number="1384">sooner it wears. That thou art my son, I have</Line>
<Line number="1385">partly thy mother's word, partly my own opinion,</Line>
<Line number="1386">but chiefly a villanous trick of thine eye and a</Line>
<Line number="1387">
foolish-hanging of thy nether lip, that doth warrant
</Line>
<Line number="1388">
me. If then thou be son to me, here lies the point;
</Line>
<Line number="1389">
why, being son to me, art thou so pointed at? Shall
</Line>
<Line number="1390">the blessed sun of heaven prove a micher and eat</Line>
<Line number="1391">blackberries? a question not to be asked. Shall</Line>
<Line number="1392">
the sun of England prove a thief and take purses? a
</Line>
<Line number="1393">question to be asked. There is a thing, Harry,</Line>
<Line number="1394">which thou hast often heard of and it is known to</Line>
<Line number="1395">many in our land by the name of pitch: this pitch,</Line>
<Line number="1396">as ancient writers do report, doth defile; so doth</Line>
<Line number="1397">the company thou keepest: for, Harry, now I do not</Line>
<Line number="1398">speak to thee in drink but in tears, not in</Line>
<Line number="1399">pleasure but in passion, not in words only, but in</Line>
<Line number="1400">woes also: and yet there is a virtuous man whom I</Line>
<Line number="1401">
have often noted in thy company, but I know not his name.
</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="1403">
A goodly portly man, i' faith, and a corpulent; of a
</Line>
<Line number="1404">cheerful look, a pleasing eye and a most noble</Line>
<Line number="1405">carriage; and, as I think, his age some fifty, or,</Line>
<Line number="1406">by'r lady, inclining to three score; and now I</Line>
<Line number="1407">remember me, his name is Falstaff: if that man</Line>
<Line number="1408">
should be lewdly given, he deceiveth me; for, Harry,
</Line>
<Line number="1409">I see virtue in his looks. If then the tree may be</Line>
<Line number="1410">
known by the fruit, as the fruit by the tree, then,
</Line>
<Line number="1411">peremptorily I speak it, there is virtue in that</Line>
<Line number="1412">Falstaff: him keep with, the rest banish. And tell</Line>
<Line number="1413">me now, thou naughty varlet, tell me, where hast</Line>
<Line number="1414">thou been this month?</Line>
</PlainText>

<Line number="1448">But to say I know more harm in him than in myself,</Line>
<Line number="1449">were to say more than I know. That he is old, the</Line>
<Line number="1450">more the pity, his white hairs do witness it; but</Line>
<Line number="1451">that he is, saving your reverence, a whoremaster,</Line>
<Line number="1452">that I utterly deny. If sack and sugar be a fault,</Line>
<Line number="1453">God help the wicked! if to be old and merry be a</Line>
<Line number="1454">
sin, then many an old host that I know is damned: if
</Line>
<Line number="1455">to be fat be to be hated, then Pharaoh's lean kine</Line>
<Line number="1456">are to be loved. No, my good lord; banish Peto,</Line>
<Line number="1457">banish Bardolph, banish Poins: but for sweet Jack</Line>
<Line number="1458">Falstaff, kind Jack Falstaff, true Jack Falstaff,</Line>
<Line number="1459">valiant Jack Falstaff, and therefore more valiant,</Line>

<Line number="1566">
O, then the earth shook to see the heavens on fire,
</Line>
<Line number="1567">And not in fear of your nativity.</Line>
<Line number="1568">Diseased nature oftentimes breaks forth</Line>
<Line number="1569">In strange eruptions; oft the teeming earth</Line>
<Line number="1570">Is with a kind of colic pinch'd and vex'd</Line>
<Line number="1571">By the imprisoning of unruly wind</Line>
<Line number="1572">Within her womb; which, for enlargement striving,</Line>
<Line number="1573">Shakes the old beldam earth and topples down</Line>
<Line number="1574">Steeples and moss-grown towers. At your birth</Line>
<Line number="1575">Our grandam earth, having this distemperature,</Line>
<Line number="1576">In passion shook.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="1577">Cousin, of many men</Line>
<Line number="1578">I do not bear these crossings. Give me leave</Line>
<Line number="1579">To tell you once again that at my birth</Line>
<Line number="1580">The front of heaven was full of fiery shapes,</Line>
<Line number="1581">The goats ran from the mountains, and the herds</Line>
<Line number="1582">Were strangely clamorous to the frighted fields.</Line>
<Line number="1583">These signs have mark'd me extraordinary;</Line>
<Line number="1584">And all the courses of my life do show</Line>
<Line number="1585">I am not in the roll of common men.</Line>
<Line number="1586">Where is he living, clipp'd in with the sea</Line>
<Line number="1587">That chides the banks of England, Scotland, Wales,</Line>
<Line number="1588">Which calls me pupil, or hath read to me?</Line>
<Line number="1589">And bring him out that is but woman's son</Line>
<Line number="1590">Can trace me in the tedious ways of art</Line>
<Line number="1591">And hold me pace in deep experiments.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="1614">The archdeacon hath divided it</Line>
<Line number="1615">Into three limits very equally:</Line>
<Line number="1616">England, from Trent and Severn hitherto,</Line>
<Line number="1617">By south and east is to my part assign'd:</Line>
<Line number="1618">All westward, Wales beyond the Severn shore,</Line>
<Line number="1619">And all the fertile land within that bound,</Line>
<Line number="1620">To Owen Glendower: and, dear coz, to you</Line>
<Line number="1621">The remnant northward, lying off from Trent.</Line>
<Line number="1622">And our indentures tripartite are drawn;</Line>
<Line number="1623">Which being sealed interchangeably,</Line>
<Line number="1624">A business that this night may execute,</Line>
<Line number="1625">To-morrow, cousin Percy, you and I</Line>
<Line number="1626">And my good Lord of Worcester will set forth</Line>
<Line number="1627">To meet your father and the Scottish power,</Line>
<Line number="1628">As is appointed us, at Shrewsbury.</Line>
<Line number="1629">My father Glendower is not ready yet,</Line>
<Line number="1630">Not shall we need his help these fourteen days.</Line>
<Line number="1631">Within that space you may have drawn together</Line>
<Line number="1632">Your tenants, friends and neighbouring gentlemen.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="1638">Methinks my moiety, north from Burton here,</Line>
<Line number="1639">In quantity equals not one of yours:</Line>
<Line number="1640">See how this river comes me cranking in,</Line>
<Line number="1641">And cuts me from the best of all my land</Line>
<Line number="1642">A huge half-moon, a monstrous cantle out.</Line>
<Line number="1643">I'll have the current in this place damm'd up;</Line>
<Line number="1644">And here the smug and silver Trent shall run</Line>
<Line number="1645">In a new channel, fair and evenly;</Line>
<Line number="1646">It shall not wind with such a deep indent,</Line>
<Line number="1647">To rob me of so rich a bottom here.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>
M0NKS M MT NR0 FRM BRTN HR IN KNTT EKLS NT ON OF YRS S H 0S RFR KMS M KRNKNK IN ANT KTS M FRM 0 BST OF AL M LNT A HJ HLFMN A MNSTRS KNTL OT IL HF 0 KRNT IN 0S PLS TMT UP ANT HR 0 SMK ANT SLFR TRNT XL RN IN A N XNL FR ANT EFNL IT XL NT WNT W0 SX A TP INTNT T RB M OF S RX A BTM HR
</PhoneticText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="1692">I cannot choose: sometime he angers me</Line>
<Line number="1693">With telling me of the mouldwarp and the ant,</Line>
<Line number="1694">Of the dreamer Merlin and his prophecies,</Line>
<Line number="1695">And of a dragon and a finless fish,</Line>
<Line number="1696">A clip-wing'd griffin and a moulten raven,</Line>
<Line number="1697">A couching lion and a ramping cat,</Line>
<Line number="1698">And such a deal of skimble-skamble stuff</Line>
<Line number="1699">As puts me from my faith. I tell you what;</Line>
<Line number="1700">He held me last night at least nine hours</Line>
<Line number="1701">In reckoning up the several devils' names</Line>
<Line number="1702">
That were his lackeys: I cried 'hum,' and 'well, go to,'
</Line>
<Line number="1703">But mark'd him not a word. O, he is as tedious</Line>
<Line number="1704">As a tired horse, a railing wife;</Line>
<Line number="1705">Worse than a smoky house: I had rather live</Line>
<Line number="1706">With cheese and garlic in a windmill, far,</Line>
<Line number="1707">Than feed on cates and have him talk to me</Line>
<Line number="1708">In any summer-house in Christendom.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Line number="1709">In faith, he is a worthy gentleman,</Line>
<Line number="1710">Exceedingly well read, and profited</Line>
<Line number="1711">In strange concealments, valiant as a lion</Line>
<Line number="1712">And as wondrous affable and as bountiful</Line>
<Line number="1713">As mines of India. Shall I tell you, cousin?</Line>
<Line number="1714">He holds your temper in a high respect</Line>
<Line number="1715">And curbs himself even of his natural scope</Line>
<Line number="1716">When you come 'cross his humour; faith, he does:</Line>
<Line number="1717">I warrant you, that man is not alive</Line>
<Line number="1718">Might so have tempted him as you have done,</Line>
<Line number="1719">Without the taste of danger and reproof:</Line>
<Line number="1720">But do not use it oft, let me entreat you.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="1721">In faith, my lord, you are too wilful-blame;</Line>
<Line number="1722">And since your coming hither have done enough</Line>
<Line number="1723">To put him quite beside his patience.</Line>
<Line number="1724">You must needs learn, lord, to amend this fault:</Line>
<Line number="1725">
Though sometimes it show greatness, courage, blood,--
</Line>
<Line number="1726">And that's the dearest grace it renders you,--</Line>
<Line number="1727">Yet oftentimes it doth present harsh rage,</Line>
<Line number="1728">Defect of manners, want of government,</Line>
<Line number="1729">Pride, haughtiness, opinion and disdain:</Line>
<Line number="1730">The least of which haunting a nobleman</Line>
<Line number="1731">Loseth men's hearts and leaves behind a stain</Line>
<Line number="1732">Upon the beauty of all parts besides,</Line>
<Line number="1733">Beguiling them of commendation.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>
IN F0 M LRT Y AR T WLFLBLM ANT SNS YR KMNK H0R HF TN ENF T PT HM KT BST HS PTNS Y MST NTS LRN LRT T AMNT 0S FLT 0 SMTMS IT X KRTNS KRJ BLT ANT 0TS 0 TRST KRS IT RNTRS Y YT OFTNTMS IT T0 PRSNT HRX RJ TFKT OF MNRS WNT OF KFRNMNT PRT HTNS OPNN ANT TSTN 0 LST OF HX HNTNK A NBLMN LS0 MNS HRTS ANT LFS BHNT A STN UPN 0 BT OF AL PRTS BSTS BKLNK 0M OF KMNTXN

<StemText>enter king henri iv princ henri and other</StemText>
<ParagraphID>639818</ParagraphID>

<Section>3</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="1822">Lords, give us leave; the Prince of Wales and I</Line>
<Line number="1823">
Must have some private conference; but be near at hand,
</Line>
<Line number="1824">For we shall presently have need of you.</Line>
<Line number="1825">[Exeunt Lords]</Line>
<Line number="1826">I know not whether God will have it so,</Line>
<Line number="1827">For some displeasing service I have done,</Line>
<Line number="1828">That, in his secret doom, out of my blood</Line>
<Line number="1829">He'll breed revengement and a scourge for me;</Line>
<Line number="1830">But thou dost in thy passages of life</Line>
<Line number="1831">Make me believe that thou art only mark'd</Line>
<Line number="1832">For the hot vengeance and the rod of heaven</Line>
<Line number="1833">To punish my mistreadings. Tell me else,</Line>
<Line number="1834">Could such inordinate and low desires,</Line>
<Line number="1835">
Such poor, such bare, such lewd, such mean attempts,
</Line>
<Line number="1836">Such barren pleasures, rude society,</Line>
<Line number="1837">As thou art match'd withal and grafted to,</Line>
<Line number="1838">Accompany the greatness of thy blood</Line>
<Line number="1839">And hold their level with thy princely heart?</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="1840">So please your majesty, I would I could</Line>
<Line number="1841">Quit all offences with as clear excuse</Line>
<Line number="1842">As well as I am doubtless I can purge</Line>
<Line number="1843">Myself of many I am charged withal:</Line>
<Line number="1844">Yet such extenuation let me beg,</Line>
<Line number="1845">As, in reproof of many tales devised,</Line>
<Line number="1846">which oft the ear of greatness needs must hear,</Line>
<Line number="1847">By smiling pick-thanks and base news-mongers,</Line>
<Line number="1848">I may, for some things true, wherein my youth</Line>
<Line number="1849">Hath faulty wander'd and irregular,</Line>
<Line number="1850">Find pardon on my true submission.</Line>
</PlainText>

<ParagraphType>b</ParagraphType>
<WorkID>henry4p1</WorkID>
<Section>3</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="1851">God pardon thee! yet let me wonder, Harry,</Line>
<Line number="1852">At thy affections, which do hold a wing</Line>
<Line number="1853">Quite from the flight of all thy ancestors.</Line>
<Line number="1854">Thy place in council thou hast rudely lost.</Line>
<Line number="1855">Which by thy younger brother is supplied,</Line>
<Line number="1856">And art almost an alien to the hearts</Line>
<Line number="1857">Of all the court and princes of my blood:</Line>
<Line number="1858">The hope and expectation of thy time</Line>
<Line number="1859">Is ruin'd, and the soul of every man</Line>
<Line number="1860">Prophetically doth forethink thy fall.</Line>
<Line number="1861">Had I so lavish of my presence been,</Line>
<Line number="1862">So common-hackney'd in the eyes of men,</Line>
<Line number="1863">So stale and cheap to vulgar company,</Line>
<Line number="1864">Opinion, that did help me to the crown,</Line>
<Line number="1865">Had still kept loyal to possession</Line>
<Line number="1866">And left me in reputeless banishment,</Line>
<Line number="1867">A fellow of no mark nor likelihood.</Line>
<Line number="1868">By being seldom seen, I could not stir</Line>
<Line number="1869">But like a comet I was wonder'd at;</Line>
<Line number="1870">That men would tell their children 'This is he;'</Line>
<Line number="1871">Others would say 'Where, which is Bolingbroke?'</Line>
<Line number="1872">And then I stole all courtesy from heaven,</Line>
<Line number="1873">And dress'd myself in such humility</Line>
<Line number="1874">That I did pluck allegiance from men's hearts,</Line>
<Line number="1875">Loud shouts and salutations from their mouths,</Line>
<Line number="1876">Even in the presence of the crowned king.</Line>
<Line number="1877">Thus did I keep my person fresh and new;</Line>
<Line number="1878">My presence, like a robe pontifical,</Line>
<Line number="1879">Ne'er seen but wonder'd at: and so my state,</Line>
<Line number="1880">Seldom but sumptuous, showed like a feast</Line>
<Line number="1881">And won by rareness such solemnity.</Line>
<Line number="1882">The skipping king, he ambled up and down</Line>
<Line number="1883">With shallow jesters and rash bavin wits,</Line>
<Line number="1884">Soon kindled and soon burnt; carded his state,</Line>
<Line number="1885">Mingled his royalty with capering fools,</Line>
<Line number="1886">Had his great name profaned with their scorns</Line>
<Line number="1887">And gave his countenance, against his name,</Line>
<Line number="1888">To laugh at gibing boys and stand the push</Line>
<Line number="1889">Of every beardless vain comparative,</Line>
<Line number="1890">Grew a companion to the common streets,</Line>
<Line number="1891">Enfeoff'd himself to popularity;</Line>
<Line number="1892">That, being daily swallow'd by men's eyes,</Line>
<Line number="1893">They surfeited with honey and began</Line>
<Line number="1894">To loathe the taste of sweetness, whereof a little</Line>
<Line number="1895">More than a little is by much too much.</Line>
<Line number="1896">So when he had occasion to be seen,</Line>
<Line number="1897">He was but as the cuckoo is in June,</Line>
<Line number="1898">Heard, not regarded; seen, but with such eyes</Line>
<Line number="1899">As, sick and blunted with community,</Line>
<Line number="1900">Afford no extraordinary gaze,</Line>
<Line number="1901">Such as is bent on sun-like majesty</Line>
<Line number="1902">When it shines seldom in admiring eyes;</Line>
<Line number="1903">But rather drowzed and hung their eyelids down,</Line>
<Line number="1904">Slept in his face and render'd such aspect</Line>
<Line number="1905">As cloudy men use to their adversaries,</Line>
<Line number="1906">Being with his presence glutted, gorged and full.</Line>
<Line number="1907">And in that very line, Harry, standest thou;</Line>
<Line number="1908">For thou has lost thy princely privilege</Line>
<Line number="1909">With vile participation: not an eye</Line>
<Line number="1910">But is a-weary of thy common sight,</Line>
<Line number="1911">Save mine, which hath desired to see thee more;</Line>
<Line number="1912">Which now doth that I would not have it do,</Line>
<Line number="1913">Make blind itself with foolish tenderness.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

</PhoneticText>

</StemText>
<ParagraphID>639822</ParagraphID>
<ParagraphNum>1932</ParagraphNum>
<CharID>henry5</CharID>
</Paragraphs>
<Paragraphs>
<ParagraphType>b</ParagraphType>
<WorkID>henry4p1</WorkID>
<Section>3</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="1916">For all the world</Line>
<Line number="1917">As thou art to this hour was Richard then</Line>
<Line number="1918">When I from France set foot at Ravenspurgh,</Line>
<Line number="1919">And even as I was then is Percy now.</Line>
<Line number="1920">Now, by my sceptre and my soul to boot,</Line>
<Line number="1921">He hath more worthy interest to the state</Line>
<Line number="1922">Than thou the shadow of succession;</Line>
<Line number="1923">For of no right, nor colour like to right,</Line>
<Line number="1924">He doth fill fields with harness in the realm,</Line>
<Line number="1925">Turns head against the lion's armed jaws,</Line>
<Line number="1926">And, being no more in debt to years than thou,</Line>
<Line number="1927">Leads ancient lords and reverend bishops on</Line>
<Line number="1928">To bloody battles and to bruising arms.</Line>
<Line number="1929">What never-dying honour hath he got</Line>
<Line number="1930">Against renowned Douglas! whose high deeds,</Line>
<Line number="1931">Whose hot incursions and great name in arms</Line>
<Line number="1932">Holds from all soldiers chief majority</Line>
<Line number="1933">And military title capital</Line>
<Line number="1934">Through all the kingdoms that acknowledge Christ:</Line>
<Line number="1935">
Thrice hath this Hotspur, Mars in swathling clothes,
</Line>
<Line number="1936">This infant warrior, in his enterprises</Line>
<Line number="1937">Discomfited great Douglas, ta'en him once,</Line>
<Line number="1938">Enlarged him and made a friend of him,</Line>
<Line number="1939">To fill the mouth of deep defiance up</Line>
<Line number="1940">And shake the peace and safety of our throne.</Line>
<Line number="1941">And what say you to this? Percy, Northumberland,</Line>
<Line number="1942">The Archbishop's grace of York, Douglas, Mortimer,</Line>
<Line number="1943">Capitulate against us and are up.</Line>
<Line number="1944">But wherefore do I tell these news to thee?</Line>
<Line number="1945">Why, Harry, do I tell thee of my foes,</Line>
<Line number="1946">Which art my near'st and dearest enemy?</Line>
<Line number="1947">Thou that art like enough, through vassal fear,</Line>
<Line number="1948">Base inclination and the start of spleen</Line>
<Line number="1949">To fight against me under Percy's pay,</Line>
<Line number="1950">To dog his heels and curtsy at his frowns,</Line>
<Line number="1951">To show how much thou art degenerate.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

<ParagraphType>b</ParagraphType>
<WorkID>henry4p1</WorkID>
<Section>3</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="1952">Do not think so; you shall not find it so:</Line>
<Line number="1953">And God forgive them that so much have sway'd</Line>
<Line number="1954">Your majesty's good thoughts away from me!</Line>
<Line number="1955">I will redeem all this on Percy's head</Line>
<Line number="1956">And in the closing of some glorious day</Line>
<Line number="1957">Be bold to tell you that I am your son;</Line>
<Line number="1958">When I will wear a garment all of blood</Line>
<Line number="1959">And stain my favours in a bloody mask,</Line>
<Line number="1960">Which, wash'd away, shall scour my shame with it:</Line>
<Line number="1961">And that shall be the day, whene'er it lights,</Line>
<Line number="1962">That this same child of honour and renown,</Line>
<Line number="1963">This gallant Hotspur, this all-praised knight,</Line>
<Line number="1964">And your unthought-of Harry chance to meet.</Line>
<Line number="1965">For every honour sitting on his helm,</Line>
<Line number="1966">Would they were multitudes, and on my head</Line>
<Line number="1967">My shames redoubled! for the time will come,</Line>
<Line number="1968">That I shall make this northern youth exchange</Line>
<Line number="1969">His glorious deeds for my indignities.</Line>
<Line number="1970">Percy is but my factor, good my lord,</Line>
<Line number="1971">To engross up glorious deeds on my behalf;</Line>
<Line number="1972">And I will call him to so strict account,</Line>
<Line number="1973">That he shall render every glory up,</Line>
<Line number="1974">Yea, even the slightest worship of his time,</Line>
<Line number="1975">Or I will tear the reckoning from his heart.</Line>
<Line number="1976">This, in the name of God, I promise here:</Line>
<Line number="1977">The which if He be pleased I shall perform,</Line>
<Line number="1978">I do beseech your majesty may salve</Line>
<Line number="1979">The long-grown wounds of my intemperance:</Line>
<Line number="1980">If not, the end of life cancels all bands;</Line>
<Line number="1981">And I will die a hundred thousand deaths</Line>
<Line number="1982">Ere break the smallest parcel of this vow.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Line number="2002">Our general forces at Bridgenorth shall meet.</Line>

No, I'll be sworn; I make as good use of it as many
</Line>
<Line number="2036">a man doth of a Death's-head or a memento mori: I</Line>
<Line number="2037">never see thy face but I think upon hell-fire and</Line>
<Line number="2038">Dives that lived in purple; for there he is in his</Line>
<Line number="2039">robes, burning, burning. If thou wert any way</Line>
<Line number="2040">
given to virtue, I would swear by thy face; my oath
</Line>
<Line number="2041">should be 'By this fire, that's God's angel:' but</Line>
<Line number="2042">
thou art altogether given over; and wert indeed, but
</Line>
<Line number="2043">for the light in thy face, the son of utter</Line>
<Line number="2044">darkness. When thou rannest up Gadshill in the</Line>
<Line number="2045">night to catch my horse, if I did not think thou</Line>
<Line number="2046">hadst been an ignis fatuus or a ball of wildfire,</Line>
<Line number="2047">there's no purchase in money. O, thou art a</Line>
<Line number="2048">perpetual triumph, an everlasting bonfire-light!</Line>
<Line number="2049">Thou hast saved me a thousand marks in links and</Line>
<Line number="2050">torches, walking with thee in the night betwixt</Line>
<Line number="2051">tavern and tavern: but the sack that thou hast</Line>
<Line number="2052">drunk me would have bought me lights as good cheap</Line>
<Line number="2053">at the dearest chandler's in Europe. I have</Line>
<Line number="2054">maintained that salamander of yours with fire any</Line>
<Line number="2055">time this two and thirty years; God reward me for</Line>
<Line number="2056">it!</Line>

</Line>
<Line number="2203">brother John; this to my Lord of Westmoreland.</Line>
<Line number="2204">[Exit Bardolph]</Line>
<Line number="2205">Go, Peto, to horse, to horse; for thou and I have</Line>
<Line number="2206">thirty miles to ride yet ere dinner time.</Line>
<Line number="2207">[Exit Peto]</Line>
<Line number="2208">Jack, meet me to-morrow in the temple hall at two</Line>
<Line number="2209">o'clock in the afternoon.</Line>
<Line number="2210">
There shalt thou know thy charge; and there receive
</Line>
<Line number="2211">Money and order for their furniture.</Line>
<Line number="2212">The land is burning; Percy stands on high;</Line>
<Line number="2213">And either we or they must lower lie.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Line number="2322">All furnish'd, all in arms;</Line>
<Line number="2323">All plumed like estridges that with the wind</Line>
<Line number="2324">Baited like eagles having lately bathed;</Line>
<Line number="2325">Glittering in golden coats, like images;</Line>
<Line number="2326">As full of spirit as the month of May,</Line>
<Line number="2327">And gorgeous as the sun at midsummer;</Line>
<Line number="2328">Wanton as youthful goats, wild as young bulls.</Line>
<Line number="2329">I saw young Harry, with his beaver on,</Line>
<Line number="2330">His cuisses on his thighs, gallantly arm'd</Line>
<Line number="2331">Rise from the ground like feather'd Mercury,</Line>
<Line number="2332">And vaulted with such ease into his seat,</Line>
<Line number="2333">As if an angel dropp'd down from the clouds,</Line>
<Line number="2334">To turn and wind a fiery Pegasus</Line>
<Line number="2335">And witch the world with noble horsemanship.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Line number="2336">No more, no more: worse than the sun in March,</Line>
<Line number="2337">This praise doth nourish agues. Let them come:</Line>
<Line number="2338">They come like sacrifices in their trim,</Line>
<Line number="2339">And to the fire-eyed maid of smoky war</Line>
<Line number="2340">All hot and bleeding will we offer them:</Line>
<Line number="2341">The mailed Mars shall on his altar sit</Line>
<Line number="2342">Up to the ears in blood. I am on fire</Line>
<Line number="2343">To hear this rich reprisal is so nigh</Line>
<Line number="2344">And yet not ours. Come, let me taste my horse,</Line>
<Line number="2345">Who is to bear me like a thunderbolt</Line>
<Line number="2346">Against the bosom of the Prince of Wales:</Line>
<Line number="2347">Harry to Harry shall, hot horse to horse,</Line>
<Line number="2348">Meet and ne'er part till one drop down a corse.</Line>
<Line number="2349">O that Glendower were come!</Line>
</PlainText>

<Chapter>2</Chapter>
<StemText>exit</StemText>
<ParagraphID>639946</ParagraphID>
<ParagraphNum>2398</ParagraphNum>
<CharID>xxx</CharID>
</Paragraphs>
<Paragraphs>
<ParagraphType>b</ParagraphType>
<WorkID>henry4p1</WorkID>
<Section>4</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="2377">If I be not ashamed of my soldiers, I am a soused</Line>
<Line number="2378">gurnet. I have misused the king's press damnably.</Line>
<Line number="2379">I have got, in exchange of a hundred and fifty</Line>
<Line number="2380">soldiers, three hundred and odd pounds. I press me</Line>
<Line number="2381">
none but good house-holders, yeoman's sons; inquire
</Line>
<Line number="2382">
me out contracted bachelors, such as had been asked
</Line>
<Line number="2383">
twice on the banns; such a commodity of warm slaves,
</Line>
<Line number="2384">as had as lieve hear the devil as a drum; such as</Line>
<Line number="2385">fear the report of a caliver worse than a struck</Line>
<Line number="2386">
fowl or a hurt wild-duck. I pressed me none but such
</Line>
<Line number="2387">toasts-and-butter, with hearts in their bellies no</Line>
<Line number="2388">bigger than pins' heads, and they have bought out</Line>
<Line number="2389">
their services; and now my whole charge consists of
</Line>
<Line number="2390">ancients, corporals, lieutenants, gentlemen of</Line>
<Line number="2391">companies, slaves as ragged as Lazarus in the</Line>
<Line number="2392">painted cloth, where the glutton's dogs licked his</Line>
<Line number="2393">sores; and such as indeed were never soldiers, but</Line>
<Line number="2394">discarded unjust serving-men, younger sons to</Line>
<Line number="2395">younger brothers, revolted tapsters and ostlers</Line>
<Line number="2396">trade-fallen, the cankers of a calm world and a</Line>
<Line number="2397">
long peace, ten times more dishonourable ragged than
</Line>
<Line number="2398">an old faced ancient: and such have I, to fill up</Line>
<Line number="2399">the rooms of them that have bought out their</Line>
<Line number="2400">
services, that you would think that I had a hundred
</Line>
<Line number="2401">and fifty tattered prodigals lately come from</Line>
<Line number="2402">swine-keeping, from eating draff and husks. A mad</Line>
<Line number="2403">
fellow met me on the way and told me I had unloaded
</Line>
<Line number="2404">
all the gibbets and pressed the dead bodies. No eye
</Line>
<Line number="2405">hath seen such scarecrows. I'll not march through</Line>
<Line number="2406">Coventry with them, that's flat: nay, and the</Line>
<Line number="2407">
villains march wide betwixt the legs, as if they had
</Line>
<Line number="2408">gyves on; for indeed I had the most of them out of</Line>
<Line number="2409">prison. There's but a shirt and a half in all my</Line>
<Line number="2410">company; and the half shirt is two napkins tacked</Line>
<Line number="2411">together and thrown over the shoulders like an</Line>
<Line number="2412">
herald's coat without sleeves; and the shirt, to say
</Line>
<Line number="2413">
the truth, stolen from my host at Saint Alban's, or
</Line>
<Line number="2414">the red-nose innkeeper of Daventry. But that's all</Line>
<Line number="2415">one; they'll find linen enough on every hedge.</Line>

<PlainText>
<Line number="2472">Come, come it nay not be. I wonder much,</Line>
<Line number="2473">Being men of such great leading as you are,</Line>
<Line number="2474">That you foresee not what impediments</Line>
<Line number="2475">Drag back our expedition: certain horse</Line>
<Line number="2476">Of my cousin Vernon's are not yet come up:</Line>
<Line number="2477">Your uncle Worcester's horse came but today;</Line>
<Line number="2478">And now their pride and mettle is asleep,</Line>
<Line number="2479">Their courage with hard labour tame and dull,</Line>
<Line number="2480">That not a horse is half the half of himself.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="2496">And God defend but still I should stand so,</Line>
<Line number="2497">So long as out of limit and true rule</Line>
<Line number="2498">You stand against anointed majesty.</Line>
<Line number="2499">But to my charge. The king hath sent to know</Line>
<Line number="2500">The nature of your griefs, and whereupon</Line>
<Line number="2501">You conjure from the breast of civil peace</Line>
<Line number="2502">Such bold hostility, teaching his duteous land</Line>
<Line number="2503">Audacious cruelty. If that the king</Line>
<Line number="2504">Have any way your good deserts forgot,</Line>
<Line number="2505">Which he confesseth to be manifold,</Line>
<Line number="2506">He bids you name your griefs; and with all speed</Line>
<Line number="2507">You shall have your desires with interest</Line>
<Line number="2508">And pardon absolute for yourself and these</Line>
<Line number="2509">Herein misled by your suggestion.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Section>4</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="2510">The king is kind; and well we know the king</Line>
<Line number="2511">Knows at what time to promise, when to pay.</Line>
<Line number="2512">My father and my uncle and myself</Line>
<Line number="2513">Did give him that same royalty he wears;</Line>
<Line number="2514">And when he was not six and twenty strong,</Line>
<Line number="2515">Sick in the world's regard, wretched and low,</Line>
<Line number="2516">A poor unminded outlaw sneaking home,</Line>
<Line number="2517">My father gave him welcome to the shore;</Line>
<Line number="2518">And when he heard him swear and vow to God</Line>
<Line number="2519">He came but to be Duke of Lancaster,</Line>
<Line number="2520">To sue his livery and beg his peace,</Line>
<Line number="2521">With tears of innocency and terms of zeal,</Line>
<Line number="2522">My father, in kind heart and pity moved,</Line>
<Line number="2523">Swore him assistance and perform'd it too.</Line>
<Line number="2524">Now when the lords and barons of the realm</Line>
<Line number="2525">Perceived Northumberland did lean to him,</Line>
<Line number="2526">The more and less came in with cap and knee;</Line>
<Line number="2527">Met him in boroughs, cities, villages,</Line>
<Line number="2528">Attended him on bridges, stood in lanes,</Line>
<Line number="2529">Laid gifts before him, proffer'd him their oaths,</Line>
<Line number="2530">Gave him their heirs, as pages follow'd him</Line>
<Line number="2531">Even at the heels in golden multitudes.</Line>
<Line number="2532">He presently, as greatness knows itself,</Line>
<Line number="2533">Steps me a little higher than his vow</Line>
<Line number="2534">Made to my father, while his blood was poor,</Line>
<Line number="2535">Upon the naked shore at Ravenspurgh;</Line>
<Line number="2536">And now, forsooth, takes on him to reform</Line>
<Line number="2537">Some certain edicts and some strait decrees</Line>
<Line number="2538">That lie too heavy on the commonwealth,</Line>
<Line number="2539">Cries out upon abuses, seems to weep</Line>
<Line number="2540">Over his country's wrongs; and by this face,</Line>
<Line number="2541">This seeming brow of justice, did he win</Line>
<Line number="2542">The hearts of all that he did angle for;</Line>
<Line number="2543">Proceeded further; cut me off the heads</Line>
<Line number="2544">Of all the favourites that the absent king</Line>
<Line number="2545">In deputation left behind him here,</Line>
<Line number="2546">When he was personal in the Irish war.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="2548">Then to the point.</Line>
<Line number="2549">In short time after, he deposed the king;</Line>
<Line number="2550">Soon after that, deprived him of his life;</Line>
<Line number="2551">And in the neck of that, task'd the whole state:</Line>
<Line number="2552">To make that worse, suffer'd his kinsman March,</Line>
<Line number="2553">Who is, if every owner were well placed,</Line>
<Line number="2554">Indeed his king, to be engaged in Wales,</Line>
<Line number="2555">There without ransom to lie forfeited;</Line>
<Line number="2556">Disgraced me in my happy victories,</Line>
<Line number="2557">Sought to entrap me by intelligence;</Line>
<Line number="2558">Rated mine uncle from the council-board;</Line>
<Line number="2559">In rage dismiss'd my father from the court;</Line>
<Line number="2560">Broke oath on oath, committed wrong on wrong,</Line>
<Line number="2561">And in conclusion drove us to seek out</Line>
<Line number="2562">This head of safety; and withal to pry</Line>
<Line number="2563">Into his title, the which we find</Line>
<Line number="2564">Too indirect for long continuance.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Section>5</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="2652">It pleased your majesty to turn your looks</Line>
<Line number="2653">Of favour from myself and all our house;</Line>
<Line number="2654">And yet I must remember you, my lord,</Line>
<Line number="2655">We were the first and dearest of your friends.</Line>
<Line number="2656">For you my staff of office did I break</Line>
<Line number="2657">In Richard's time; and posted day and night</Line>
<Line number="2658">to meet you on the way, and kiss your hand,</Line>
<Line number="2659">When yet you were in place and in account</Line>
<Line number="2660">Nothing so strong and fortunate as I.</Line>
<Line number="2661">It was myself, my brother and his son,</Line>
<Line number="2662">That brought you home and boldly did outdare</Line>
<Line number="2663">The dangers of the time. You swore to us,</Line>
<Line number="2664">And you did swear that oath at Doncaster,</Line>
<Line number="2665">That you did nothing purpose 'gainst the state;</Line>
<Line number="2666">Nor claim no further than your new-fall'n right,</Line>
<Line number="2667">The seat of Gaunt, dukedom of Lancaster:</Line>
<Line number="2668">To this we swore our aid. But in short space</Line>
<Line number="2669">It rain'd down fortune showering on your head;</Line>
<Line number="2670">And such a flood of greatness fell on you,</Line>
<Line number="2671">What with our help, what with the absent king,</Line>
<Line number="2672">What with the injuries of a wanton time,</Line>
<Line number="2673">The seeming sufferances that you had borne,</Line>
<Line number="2674">And the contrarious winds that held the king</Line>
<Line number="2675">So long in his unlucky Irish wars</Line>
<Line number="2676">That all in England did repute him dead:</Line>
<Line number="2677">And from this swarm of fair advantages</Line>
<Line number="2678">You took occasion to be quickly woo'd</Line>
<Line number="2679">To gripe the general sway into your hand;</Line>
<Line number="2680">Forget your oath to us at Doncaster;</Line>
<Line number="2681">And being fed by us you used us so</Line>
<Line number="2682">As that ungentle hull, the cuckoo's bird,</Line>
<Line number="2683">Useth the sparrow; did oppress our nest;</Line>
<Line number="2684">Grew by our feeding to so great a bulk</Line>
<Line number="2685">That even our love durst not come near your sight</Line>
<Line number="2686">For fear of swallowing; but with nimble wing</Line>
<Line number="2687">We were enforced, for safety sake, to fly</Line>
<Line number="2688">Out of sight and raise this present head;</Line>
<Line number="2689">Whereby we stand opposed by such means</Line>
<Line number="2690">As you yourself have forged against yourself</Line>
<Line number="2691">By unkind usage, dangerous countenance,</Line>
<Line number="2692">And violation of all faith and troth</Line>
<Line number="2693">Sworn to us in your younger enterprise.</Line>
</PlainText>
<PhoneticText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="2694">These things indeed you have articulate,</Line>
<Line number="2695">Proclaim'd at market-crosses, read in churches,</Line>
<Line number="2696">To face the garment of rebellion</Line>
<Line number="2697">With some fine colour that may please the eye</Line>
<Line number="2698">Of fickle changelings and poor discontents,</Line>
<Line number="2699">Which gape and rub the elbow at the news</Line>
<Line number="2700">Of hurlyburly innovation:</Line>
<Line number="2701">And never yet did insurrection want</Line>
<Line number="2702">Such water-colours to impaint his cause;</Line>
<Line number="2703">Nor moody beggars, starving for a time</Line>
<Line number="2704">Of pellmell havoc and confusion.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="2705">In both your armies there is many a soul</Line>
<Line number="2706">Shall pay full dearly for this encounter,</Line>
<Line number="2707">If once they join in trial. Tell your nephew,</Line>
<Line number="2708">The Prince of Wales doth join with all the world</Line>
<Line number="2709">In praise of Henry Percy: by my hopes,</Line>
<Line number="2710">This present enterprise set off his head,</Line>
<Line number="2711">I do not think a braver gentleman,</Line>
<Line number="2712">More active-valiant or more valiant-young,</Line>
<Line number="2713">More daring or more bold, is now alive</Line>
<Line number="2714">To grace this latter age with noble deeds.</Line>
<Line number="2715">For my part, I may speak it to my shame,</Line>
<Line number="2716">I have a truant been to chivalry;</Line>
<Line number="2717">And so I hear he doth account me too;</Line>
<Line number="2718">Yet this before my father's majesty--</Line>
<Line number="2719">I am content that he shall take the odds</Line>
<Line number="2720">Of his great name and estimation,</Line>
<Line number="2721">And will, to save the blood on either side,</Line>
<Line number="2722">Try fortune with him in a single fight.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="2723">And, Prince of Wales, so dare we venture thee,</Line>
<Line number="2724">Albeit considerations infinite</Line>
<Line number="2725">Do make against it. No, good Worcester, no,</Line>
<Line number="2726">We love our people well; even those we love</Line>
<Line number="2727">That are misled upon your cousin's part;</Line>
<Line number="2728">And, will they take the offer of our grace,</Line>
<Line number="2729">Both he and they and you, every man</Line>
<Line number="2730">Shall be my friend again and I'll be his:</Line>
<Line number="2731">So tell your cousin, and bring me word</Line>
<Line number="2732">What he will do: but if he will not yield,</Line>
<Line number="2733">Rebuke and dread correction wait on us</Line>
<Line number="2734">And they shall do their office. So, be gone;</Line>
<Line number="2735">We will not now be troubled with reply:</Line>
<Line number="2736">We offer fair; take it advisedly.</Line>
</PlainText>

<PlainText>
<Line number="2752">
'Tis not due yet; I would be loath to pay him before
</Line>
<Line number="2753">his day. What need I be so forward with him that</Line>
<Line number="2754">
calls not on me? Well, 'tis no matter; honour pricks
</Line>
<Line number="2755">me on. Yea, but how if honour prick me off when I</Line>
<Line number="2756">come on? how then? Can honour set to a leg? no: or</Line>
<Line number="2757">an arm? no: or take away the grief of a wound? no.</Line>
<Line number="2758">Honour hath no skill in surgery, then? no. What is</Line>
<Line number="2759">honour? a word. What is in that word honour? what</Line>
<Line number="2760">
is that honour? air. A trim reckoning! Who hath it?
</Line>
<Line number="2761">he that died o' Wednesday. Doth he feel it? no.</Line>
<Line number="2762">Doth he hear it? no. 'Tis insensible, then. Yea,</Line>
<Line number="2763">to the dead. But will it not live with the living?</Line>
<Line number="2764">no. Why? detraction will not suffer it. Therefore</Line>
<Line number="2765">
I'll none of it. Honour is a mere scutcheon: and so
</Line>
<Line number="2766">ends my catechism.</Line>
</PlainText>

<Section>5</Section>
<PlainText>
<Line number="2772">Then are we all undone.</Line>
<Line number="2773">It is not possible, it cannot be,</Line>
<Line number="2774">The king should keep his word in loving us;</Line>
<Line number="2775">He will suspect us still and find a time</Line>
<Line number="2776">To punish this offence in other faults:</Line>
<Line number="2777">
Suspicion all our lives shall be stuck full of eyes;
</Line>
<Line number="2778">For treason is but trusted like the fox,</Line>
<Line number="2779">Who, ne'er so tame, so cherish'd and lock'd up,</Line>
<Line number="2780">Will have a wild trick of his ancestors.</Line>
<Line number="2781">Look how we can, or sad or merrily,</Line>
<Line number="2782">Interpretation will misquote our looks,</Line>
<Line number="2783">And we shall feed like oxen at a stall,</Line>
<Line number="2784">The better cherish'd, still the nearer death.</Line>
<Line number="2785">My nephew's trespass may be well forgot;</Line>
<Line number="2786">it hath the excuse of youth and heat of blood,</Line>
<Line number="2787">And an adopted name of privilege,</Line>
<Line number="2788">A hair-brain'd Hotspur, govern'd by a spleen:</Line>
<Line number="2789">All his offences live upon my head</Line>
<Line number="2790">And on his father's; we did train him on,</Line>
<Line number="2791">And, his corruption being ta'en from us,</Line>
<Line number="2792">We, as the spring of all, shall pay for all.</Line>
<Line number="2793">Therefore, good cousin, let not Harry know,</Line>
<Line number="2794">In any case, the offer of the king.</Line>
</PlainText>
